<img alt="Category" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/thumb/4/48/Folder_Hexagonal_Icon.svg/16px-Folder_Hexagonal_Icon.svg.png" title="Category" width="16" height="14" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/thumb/4/48/Folder_Hexagonal_Icon.svg/24px-Folder_Hexagonal_Icon.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/thumb/4/48/Folder_Hexagonal_Icon.svg/32px-Folder_Hexagonal_Icon.svg.png 2x" data-file-width="36" data-file-height="31" /> <a href="/wiki/Category:Antisemitism" title="Category:Antisemitism">Category</a></td></tr><tr><td style="text-align:right;font-size:115%;padding-top: 0.6em;"><div class="plainlinks hlist navbar mini"><ul><li class="nv-view"><a href="/wiki/Template:Antisemitism" title="Template:Antisemitism"><abbr title="View this template">v</abbr></a></li><li class="nv-talk"><a href="/wiki/Template_talk:Antisemitism" title="Template talk:Antisemitism"><abbr title="Discuss this template">t</abbr></a></li><li class="nv-edit"><a class="external text" href="//en.wikipedia.org/w/index.php?title=Template:Antisemitism&amp;action=edit"><abbr title="Edit this template">e</abbr></a></li></ul></div></td></tr></tbody></table>
<p><b>Neo-Nazism</b> consists of post-<a href="/wiki/World_War_II" title="World War II">World War II</a> militant social or political movements seeking to revive and implement the ideology of <a href="/wiki/Nazism" title="Nazism">Nazism</a>. Neo-Nazis seek to employ their ideology to promote hatred and attack minorities, or in some cases to create a fascist political state.<sup id="cite_ref-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-1">&#91;1&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-2">&#91;2&#93;</a></sup> It is a global phenomenon, with organized representation in many countries and international networks. It borrows elements from Nazi doctrine, including <a href="/wiki/Ultranationalism" title="Ultranationalism">ultranationalism</a>, <a href="/wiki/Racism" title="Racism">racism</a> up to <a href="/wiki/Xenophobia" title="Xenophobia">xenophobia</a>, <a href="/wiki/Ableism" title="Ableism">ableism</a>, <a href="/wiki/Homophobia" title="Homophobia">homophobia</a>, <a href="/wiki/Anti-Romanyism" class="mw-redirect" title="Anti-Romanyism">anti-Romanyism</a>, <a href="/wiki/Antisemitism" title="Antisemitism">antisemitism</a>, <a href="/wiki/Anti-communism" title="Anti-communism">anti-communism</a> and initiating the <a href="/wiki/Fourth_Reich" title="Fourth Reich">Fourth Reich</a>. <a href="/wiki/Holocaust_denial" title="Holocaust denial">Holocaust denial</a> is a common feature, as is the incorporation of <a href="/wiki/Nazi_symbolism" title="Nazi symbolism">Nazi symbols</a> and admiration of <a href="/wiki/Adolf_Hitler" title="Adolf Hitler">Adolf Hitler</a>.
</p><p>In some <a href="/wiki/Europe" title="Europe">European</a> and <a href="/wiki/Latin_America" title="Latin America">Latin American</a> countries, laws prohibit the expression of pro-Nazi, racist, anti-Semitic, or homophobic views. Many Nazi-related symbols are banned in European countries (especially <a href="/wiki/Strafgesetzbuch_section_86a" title="Strafgesetzbuch section 86a">Germany</a>) in an effort to curtail neo-Nazism.<sup id="cite_ref-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-3">&#91;3&#93;</a></sup>
</p>

<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Definition">Definition</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Neo-Nazism&amp;action=edit&amp;section=1" title="Edit section: Definition">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<p>The term Neo-Nazism describes any post-<a href="/wiki/World_War_II" title="World War II">World War II</a> militant, social or political movements seeking to revive the ideology of <a href="/wiki/Nazism" title="Nazism">Nazism</a> in whole or in part.<sup id="cite_ref-4" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-4">&#91;4&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-5" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-5">&#91;5&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>The term <i>neo-Nazism</i> can also refer to the <a href="/wiki/Ideology" title="Ideology">ideology</a> of these movements, which may borrow elements from Nazi doctrine, including <a href="/wiki/Ultranationalism" title="Ultranationalism">ultranationalism</a>, <a href="/wiki/Anti-communism" title="Anti-communism">anti-communism</a>, <a href="/wiki/Racism" title="Racism">racism</a>, <a href="/wiki/Ableism" title="Ableism">ableism</a>, <a href="/wiki/Xenophobia" title="Xenophobia">xenophobia</a>, <a href="/wiki/Homophobia" title="Homophobia">homophobia</a>, <a href="/wiki/Anti-Romanyism" class="mw-redirect" title="Anti-Romanyism">anti-Romanyism</a>, <a href="/wiki/Antisemitism" title="Antisemitism">antisemitism</a>, up to initiating the <a href="/wiki/Fourth_Reich" title="Fourth Reich">Fourth Reich</a>. <a href="/wiki/Holocaust_denial" title="Holocaust denial">Holocaust denial</a> is a common feature, as is the incorporation of <a href="/wiki/Nazi_symbolism" title="Nazi symbolism">Nazi symbols</a> and admiration of <a href="/wiki/Adolf_Hitler" title="Adolf Hitler">Adolf Hitler</a>. 
</p><p>Neo-Nazism is considered a particular form of <a href="/wiki/Far-right_politics" title="Far-right politics">far-right politics</a> and right-wing extremism.<sup id="cite_ref-6" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-6">&#91;6&#93;</a></sup> 
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Hyperborean_racial_doctrine">Hyperborean racial doctrine</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Neo-Nazism&amp;action=edit&amp;section=2" title="Edit section: Hyperborean racial doctrine">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>Neo-Nazi writers have posited a spiritual, esoteric doctrine of <a href="/wiki/Race_(human_categorization)" title="Race (human categorization)">race</a>, which moves beyond the primarily <a href="/wiki/Darwinian" class="mw-redirect" title="Darwinian">Darwinian</a>-inspired materialist <a href="/wiki/Scientific_racism" title="Scientific racism">scientific racism</a> popular mainly in the Anglosphere during the 20th century. Figures influential in the development of neo-Nazi racism, such as <a href="/wiki/Miguel_Serrano" title="Miguel Serrano">Miguel Serrano</a> and <a href="/wiki/Julius_Evola" title="Julius Evola">Julius Evola</a>,<sup id="cite_ref-7" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-7">&#91;7&#93;</a></sup> claim that the <a href="/wiki/Hyperborean" class="mw-redirect" title="Hyperborean">Hyperborean</a> ancestors of the <a href="/wiki/Aryans" class="mw-redirect" title="Aryans">Aryans</a> were in the distant past, far higher beings than their current state, having suffered from "involution" due to mixing with the "Telluric" peoples; supposed creations of the <a href="/wiki/Demiurge" title="Demiurge">Demiurge</a>. Within this theory, if the "Aryans" are to return to the <a href="/wiki/Golden_Age" title="Golden Age">Golden Age</a> of the distant past, they need to awaken the memory of the blood. An <a href="/wiki/Extraterrestrial_life" title="Extraterrestrial life">extraterrestrial</a> origin of the Hyperboreans is often claimed. These theories draw influence from <a href="/wiki/Gnosticism" title="Gnosticism">Gnosticism</a> and <a href="/wiki/Tantrism" class="mw-redirect" title="Tantrism">Tantrism</a>, building on the work of the <a href="/wiki/Ahnenerbe" title="Ahnenerbe">Ahnenerbe</a>. Within this racist theory, Jews are held up as the antithesis of nobility, purity and beauty.
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Ecology_and_environmentalism">Ecology and environmentalism</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Neo-Nazism&amp;action=edit&amp;section=3" title="Edit section: Ecology and environmentalism">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>Neo-Nazism generally aligns itself with a <a href="/wiki/Blood_and_soil" class="mw-redirect" title="Blood and soil">blood and soil</a> variation of <a href="/wiki/Environmentalism" title="Environmentalism">environmentalism</a>, which has themes in common with <a href="/wiki/Deep_ecology" title="Deep ecology">deep ecology</a>, the <a href="/wiki/Organic_movement" title="Organic movement">organic movement</a> and <a href="/wiki/Animal_protectionism" title="Animal protectionism">animal protectionism</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-8" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-8">&#91;8&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-9" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-9">&#91;9&#93;</a></sup> This tendency, sometimes called "<a href="/wiki/Ecofascism" title="Ecofascism">ecofascism</a>", was represented in the original German National Socialism by <a href="/wiki/Richard_Walther_Darr%C3%A9" title="Richard Walther Darré">Richard Walther Darré</a> who was the <a href="/wiki/Reichsminister_of_Food" class="mw-redirect" title="Reichsminister of Food">Reichsminister of Food</a> from 1933 until 1942.<sup id="cite_ref-10" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-10">&#91;10&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="History">History</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Neo-Nazism&amp;action=edit&amp;section=4" title="Edit section: History">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<h3><span id="Germany.2C_Austria_1945.E2.80.931950s"></span><span class="mw-headline" id="Germany,_Austria_1945–1950s">Germany, Austria 1945–1950s</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Neo-Nazism&amp;action=edit&amp;section=5" title="Edit section: Germany, Austria 1945–1950s">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>Following the defeat of <a href="/wiki/Nazi_Germany" title="Nazi Germany">Nazi Germany</a>, the political ideology of the ruling party, Nazism, was in complete disarray. The final leader of the <a href="/wiki/Nazi_Party" title="Nazi Party">National Socialist German Workers' Party</a> was <a href="/wiki/Martin_Bormann" title="Martin Bormann">Martin Bormann</a>. He died on 2 May 1945 during the <a href="/wiki/Battle_of_Berlin" title="Battle of Berlin">Battle of Berlin</a>, but the <a href="/wiki/Soviet_Union" title="Soviet Union">Soviet Union</a> did not reveal his death to the rest of the world, and his ultimate fate remained a mystery for many years. <a href="/wiki/Conspiracy_theories_about_Adolf_Hitler%27s_death" title="Conspiracy theories about Adolf Hitler&#39;s death">Conspiracy theories</a> emerged about Hitler himself, that he had secretly survived the war and fled to South America or elsewhere.
</p><p>The <a href="/wiki/Allied_Control_Council" title="Allied Control Council">Allied Control Council</a> officially dissolved the NSDAP on 10 October 1945, marking the end of "Old" National Socialism. A process of <a href="/wiki/Denazification" title="Denazification">denazification</a> began, and the <a href="/wiki/Nuremberg_trials" title="Nuremberg trials">Nuremberg trials</a> took place, where many major leaders and ideologues were condemned to death by October 1946, others committed suicide.
</p>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Otto_Ernst_Remer_portrait.JPG" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/44/Otto_Ernst_Remer_portrait.JPG/220px-Otto_Ernst_Remer_portrait.JPG" width="220" height="313" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/44/Otto_Ernst_Remer_portrait.JPG/330px-Otto_Ernst_Remer_portrait.JPG 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/44/Otto_Ernst_Remer_portrait.JPG/440px-Otto_Ernst_Remer_portrait.JPG 2x" data-file-width="443" data-file-height="630" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Otto_Ernst_Remer_portrait.JPG" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div><a href="/wiki/Otto_Ernst_Remer" title="Otto Ernst Remer">Otto Ernst Remer</a>, leader of the postwar <a href="/wiki/Socialist_Reich_Party" title="Socialist Reich Party">Socialist Reich Party</a>.</div></div></div>
<p>In both the East and West, surviving ex-party members and military veterans assimilated to the new reality and had no interest in constructing a "neo-Nazism."<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">&#91;<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (December 2017)">citation needed</span></a></i>&#93;</sup> However, during the <a href="/wiki/West_German_federal_election,_1949" title="West German federal election, 1949">1949 elections</a> a number of National Socialist advocates such as <a href="/wiki/Fritz_R%C3%B6ssler" title="Fritz Rössler">Fritz Rössler</a> had infiltrated the national conservative <i><a href="/wiki/Deutsche_Rechtspartei" title="Deutsche Rechtspartei">Deutsche Rechtspartei</a></i>, which had 5 members elected. Rössler and others left to found the more radical <a href="/wiki/Socialist_Reich_Party" title="Socialist Reich Party">Socialist Reich Party</a> under <a href="/wiki/Otto_Ernst_Remer" title="Otto Ernst Remer">Otto Ernst Remer</a>. At the onset of the <a href="/wiki/Cold_War" title="Cold War">Cold War</a>, the SRP favoured the Soviet Union over the United States.<sup id="cite_ref-remer_11-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-remer-11">&#91;11&#93;</a></sup> 
</p><p>In <a href="/wiki/Austria" title="Austria">Austria</a> national independence had been restored, and the <i><a href="/wiki/Verbotsgesetz_1947" title="Verbotsgesetz 1947">Verbotsgesetz 1947</a></i> explicitly criminalised the NSDAP and any attempt at restoration. 
West Germany adopted a similar law to target parties it defined as anti-constitutional; Article 21 Paragraph 2 in the <a href="/wiki/Basic_Law_for_the_Federal_Republic_of_Germany" title="Basic Law for the Federal Republic of Germany">Basic Law</a>, banning the Socialist Reich Party in 1952 for being opposed to <a href="/wiki/Liberal_democracy" title="Liberal democracy">liberal democracy</a>.
</p><p>As a consequence some members of the nascent movement of German neo-Nazism joined the <i><a href="/wiki/Deutsche_Reichspartei" title="Deutsche Reichspartei">Deutsche Reichspartei</a></i> of which <a href="/wiki/Hans-Ulrich_Rudel" title="Hans-Ulrich Rudel">Hans-Ulrich Rudel</a> was the most prominent figure. Younger members founded the <i><a href="/wiki/Wiking-Jugend" title="Wiking-Jugend">Wiking-Jugend</a></i> modeled after the <a href="/wiki/Hitler_Youth" title="Hitler Youth">Hitler Youth</a>. The <i>Deutsche Reichspartei</i> stood for elections from 1953 until 1961 fetching around 1% of the vote each time.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">&#91;<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (December 2017)">citation needed</span></a></i>&#93;</sup> Rudel befriended French-born <a href="/wiki/Savitri_Devi" title="Savitri Devi">Savitri Devi</a>, who was a proponent of <a href="/wiki/Esoteric_Nazism" title="Esoteric Nazism">Esoteric Nazism</a>. In the 1950s she wrote a number of books, such as <i><a href="/wiki/Pilgrimage_(book)" title="Pilgrimage (book)">Pilgrimage</a></i> (1958), which concerns prominent Third Reich sites, and <i><a href="/wiki/The_Lightning_and_the_Sun" title="The Lightning and the Sun">The Lightning and the Sun</a></i> (1958), in which she claims that Adolf Hitler was an avatar of the God <a href="/wiki/Vishnu" title="Vishnu">Vishnu</a>. She was not alone in this reorientation of National Socialism towards its <a href="/wiki/Thule_Society" title="Thule Society">Thulean</a>-roots; the <i><a href="/wiki/Artgemeinschaft" title="Artgemeinschaft">Artgemeinschaft</a></i>, founded by former SS member Wilhelm Kusserow, attempted to promote a new <a href="/wiki/Paganism" title="Paganism">paganism</a>.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">&#91;<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (December 2017)">citation needed</span></a></i>&#93;</sup> 
In the <a href="/wiki/German_Democratic_Republic" class="mw-redirect" title="German Democratic Republic">German Democratic Republic</a> a former member of <a href="/wiki/Sturmabteilung" title="Sturmabteilung">SA</a>, <a href="/wiki/Wilhelm_Adam" title="Wilhelm Adam">Wilhelm Adam</a>, founded the <a href="/wiki/National_Democratic_Party_of_Germany_(East_Germany)" title="National Democratic Party of Germany (East Germany)">National Democratic Party of Germany (East Germany)</a>. It reached out to those attracted by the Nazi Party before 1945 and provide them with a political outlet, so that they would not be tempted to support the far-right again or turn to the anti-communist Western Allies.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">&#91;<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (December 2017)">citation needed</span></a></i>&#93;</sup> Stalin wanted to use them to create a new pro-Soviet and anti-Western strain in German politics.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">&#91;<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (December 2017)">citation needed</span></a></i>&#93;</sup> According to top Soviet diplomat Vladimir Semyonov, Stalin even suggested that they could be allowed to continue publishing their own newspaper, <a href="/wiki/V%C3%B6lkischer_Beobachter" title="Völkischer Beobachter">Völkischer Beobachter</a>. While in Austria, former SS member Wilhelm Lang founded an esoteric group known as the <a href="/wiki/Landig_Group" title="Landig Group">Vienna Lodge</a>; he popularised <a href="/wiki/Nazism_and_occultism" class="mw-redirect" title="Nazism and occultism">nazism and occultism</a> such as the <a href="/wiki/Black_Sun_(occult_symbol)" class="mw-redirect" title="Black Sun (occult symbol)">Black Sun</a> and ideas of Third Reich survival colonies below the polar ice caps.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">&#91;<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (December 2017)">citation needed</span></a></i>&#93;</sup>
</p>
<div class="thumb tleft"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Otto_Strasser.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/74/Otto_Strasser.jpg/220px-Otto_Strasser.jpg" width="220" height="220" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/7/74/Otto_Strasser.jpg 1.5x" data-file-width="320" data-file-height="320" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Otto_Strasser.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div><a href="/wiki/Otto_Strasser" title="Otto Strasser">Otto Strasser</a>, leader of the <a href="/wiki/German_Social_Union_(West_Germany)" title="German Social Union (West Germany)">German Social Union</a>, returned from exile to Germany in the mid-1950s.</div></div></div>
<p>With the onset of the <a href="/wiki/Cold_War" title="Cold War">Cold War</a> the allied forces had lost interest in prosecuting anyone as part of the denazification.<sup id="cite_ref-Evans-2008_12-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Evans-2008-12">&#91;12&#93;</a></sup> In the mid-1950s this new political environment allowed <a href="/wiki/Otto_Strasser" title="Otto Strasser">Otto Strasser</a>, an NS activist on the left of the NSDAP, who had founded the <a href="/wiki/Black_Front" title="Black Front">Black Front</a> to return from exile. In 1956, Strasser founded the <a href="/wiki/German_Social_Union_(West_Germany)" title="German Social Union (West Germany)">German Social Union</a> as a Black Front successor, promoting a <a href="/wiki/Strasserite" class="mw-redirect" title="Strasserite">Strasserite</a> "nationalist and socialist" policy, which dissolved in 1962 due to lack of support. Other Third Reich associated groups were the <a href="/wiki/HIAG" title="HIAG">HIAG</a> and <a href="/wiki/Stille_Hilfe" title="Stille Hilfe">Stille Hilfe</a> dedicated to advancing the interests of <a href="/wiki/Waffen-SS" title="Waffen-SS">Waffen-SS</a> veterans and rehabilitating them into the new democratic society. However, they did not claim to be attempting to restore National Socialism, instead working with the <a href="/wiki/Social_democrats" class="mw-redirect" title="Social democrats">social democrats</a> and <a href="/wiki/Christian_democrats" class="mw-redirect" title="Christian democrats">Christian democrats</a>.
</p><p>Many bureaucrats who served under the Third Reich continued to serve in German administration after the war. According to the <a href="/wiki/Simon_Wiesenthal_Center" title="Simon Wiesenthal Center">Simon Wiesenthal Center</a>, many of the more than 90,000 Nazi war criminals recorded in German files were serving in positions of prominence under Chancellor <a href="/wiki/Konrad_Adenauer" title="Konrad Adenauer">Konrad Adenauer</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-Simon_Wiesenthal_Center_13-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Simon_Wiesenthal_Center-13">&#91;13&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Hartmann_14-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Hartmann-14">&#91;14&#93;</a></sup> Not until the 1960s were the former concentration camp personnel prosecuted by <a href="/wiki/West_Germany" title="West Germany">West Germany</a> in the <a href="/wiki/Belzec_trial" title="Belzec trial">Belzec trial</a>, <a href="/wiki/Frankfurt_Auschwitz_trials" title="Frankfurt Auschwitz trials">Frankfurt Auschwitz trials</a>, <a href="/wiki/Treblinka_trials" title="Treblinka trials">Treblinka trials</a>, <a href="/wiki/Che%C5%82mno_trials" title="Chełmno trials">Chełmno trials</a>, and the <a href="/wiki/Sobib%C3%B3r_trial" title="Sobibór trial">Sobibór trial</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-Rückerl-1972_15-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Rückerl-1972-15">&#91;15&#93;</a></sup> However, the government had passed laws prohibiting National Socialists from publicly expressing their beliefs.
</p>
<h3><span id=".22Universal_National_Socialism.22.2C_1950s.E2.80.931970s"></span><span class="mw-headline" id="&quot;Universal_National_Socialism&quot;,_1950s–1970s">"Universal National Socialism", 1950s–1970s</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Neo-Nazism&amp;action=edit&amp;section=6" title="Edit section: &quot;Universal National Socialism&quot;, 1950s–1970s">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>Neo-Nazism found expression outside of Germany, including in countries who fought against the Third Reich during the Second World War, and sometimes adopted Pan-European or "Universal" characteristics, beyond the parameters of <a href="/wiki/German_nationalism" title="German nationalism">German nationalism</a>.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">&#91;<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (December 2017)">citation needed</span></a></i>&#93;</sup> The two main tendencies, with differing styles and even worldviews, were the followers of the American <a href="/wiki/Francis_Parker_Yockey" title="Francis Parker Yockey">Francis Parker Yockey</a>, who was fundamentally anti-American and advocated for a <a href="/wiki/Pan-European_nationalism" title="Pan-European nationalism">Pan-European nationalism</a> and those of <a href="/wiki/George_Lincoln_Rockwell" title="George Lincoln Rockwell">George Lincoln Rockwell</a> an <a href="/wiki/American_conservative" class="mw-redirect" title="American conservative">American conservative</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-16" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-16">&#91;nb 1&#93;</a></sup><sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">&#91;<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (December 2017)">citation needed</span></a></i>&#93;</sup>
</p><p>Yockey, a neo-Spenglerian author, had written <i>Imperium: The Philosophy of History and Politics</i> (1949) dedicated to "the hero of the twentieth century" (namely, Adolf Hitler) and founded the <a href="/wiki/European_Liberation_Front" title="European Liberation Front">European Liberation Front</a>. He was interested more in the destiny of Europe; to this end, he advocated a <a href="/wiki/National_Bolshevik" class="mw-redirect" title="National Bolshevik">National Bolshevik</a>-esque <a href="/wiki/Red%E2%80%93green%E2%80%93brown_alliance" title="Red–green–brown alliance">red-brown alliance</a> against <a href="/wiki/American_culture" class="mw-redirect" title="American culture">American culture</a> and influenced 1960s figures such as SS-veteran <a href="/wiki/Jean-Fran%C3%A7ois_Thiriart" title="Jean-François Thiriart">Jean-François Thiriart</a>. Yockey was also fond of <a href="/wiki/Arab_nationalism" title="Arab nationalism">Arab nationalism</a>, in particular <a href="/wiki/Gamal_Abdel_Nasser" title="Gamal Abdel Nasser">Gamal Abdel Nasser</a>, as well as this he saw <a href="/wiki/Fidel_Castro" title="Fidel Castro">Fidel Castro</a>'s  <a href="/wiki/Cuban_Revolution" title="Cuban Revolution">Cuban Revolution</a> as a positive and visited officials there. Yockey's views impressed Otto Ernst Remer and the radical traditionalist philosopher <a href="/wiki/Julius_Evola" title="Julius Evola">Julius Evola</a>. He was constantly hounded by the <a href="/wiki/FBI" class="mw-redirect" title="FBI">FBI</a> and was eventually arrested in 1960, before committing suicide. Domestically, Yockey's biggest sympathisers were the <a href="/wiki/National_Renaissance_Party_(United_States)" title="National Renaissance Party (United States)">National Renaissance Party</a>, including <a href="/wiki/James_H._Madole" title="James H. Madole">James H. Madole</a>, <a href="/wiki/H._Keith_Thompson" title="H. Keith Thompson">H. Keith Thompson</a> and <a href="/wiki/Eustace_Mullins" title="Eustace Mullins">Eustace Mullins</a> (<i>protégé</i> of <a href="/wiki/Ezra_Pound" title="Ezra Pound">Ezra Pound</a>) and the <a href="/wiki/Liberty_Lobby" title="Liberty Lobby">Liberty Lobby</a> of <a href="/wiki/Willis_Carto" title="Willis Carto">Willis Carto</a>.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">&#91;<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (December 2017)">citation needed</span></a></i>&#93;</sup>
</p>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:202px;"><a href="/wiki/File:GeorgeLincolnRockwell.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/e/e5/GeorgeLincolnRockwell.jpg" width="200" height="256" class="thumbimage" data-file-width="200" data-file-height="256" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:GeorgeLincolnRockwell.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div><a href="/wiki/George_Lincoln_Rockwell" title="George Lincoln Rockwell">George Lincoln Rockwell</a>, founder of the <a href="/wiki/American_Nazi_Party" title="American Nazi Party">American Nazi Party</a> and progenitor of subsequent uniformed neo-Nazi groups.</div></div></div>
<p>Rockwell, an American conservative, was first politicised by <a href="/wiki/Anti-communism" title="Anti-communism">anti-communism</a> and opposed to <a href="/wiki/Racial_integration" title="Racial integration">racial integration</a>, before becoming <a href="/wiki/Anti-Jewish" class="mw-redirect" title="Anti-Jewish">anti-Jewish</a>. In response to his opponents calling him a "Nazi", he theatrically appropriated the aesthetic elements of the NSDAP, to "own" the intended insult. In 1959, Rockwell founded the <a href="/wiki/American_Nazi_Party" title="American Nazi Party">American Nazi Party</a> and instructed his members to dress in imitation <a href="/wiki/Sturmabteilung" title="Sturmabteilung">SA</a>-style brown shirts, while flying the flag of the Third Reich. In contrast to Yockey, he was pro-American and cooperated with FBI requests, despite the party being targeted under <a href="/wiki/COINTELPRO" title="COINTELPRO">COINTELPRO</a> due to the mistaken belief that they were agents of Nasser's Egypt during a brief intelligence "brown scare."<sup id="cite_ref-17" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-17">&#91;nb 2&#93;</a></sup> Later leaders of American <a href="/wiki/White_nationalism" title="White nationalism">white nationalism</a> came to politics through the ANP; including a teenage <a href="/wiki/David_Duke" title="David Duke">David Duke</a> and <a href="/wiki/William_Luther_Pierce" title="William Luther Pierce">William Luther Pierce</a> of the <a href="/wiki/National_Alliance_(United_States)" title="National Alliance (United States)">National Alliance</a>, although they soon distanced themselves from explicit self-identification with neo-Nazism.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">&#91;<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (December 2017)">citation needed</span></a></i>&#93;</sup>
</p><p>In 1961,  the <a href="/wiki/World_Union_of_National_Socialists" title="World Union of National Socialists">World Union of National Socialists</a> was founded by Rockwell and <a href="/wiki/Colin_Jordan" title="Colin Jordan">Colin Jordan</a> of the British <a href="/wiki/National_Socialist_Movement_(UK,_1962)" title="National Socialist Movement (UK, 1962)">National Socialist Movement</a>, adopting the <i><a href="/wiki/Cotswold_Declaration" class="mw-redirect" title="Cotswold Declaration">Cotswold Declaration</a>.</i> French socialite <a href="/wiki/Fran%C3%A7oise_Dior" title="Françoise Dior">Françoise Dior</a> was involved romantically with Jordan and his deputy <a href="/wiki/John_Tyndall_(politician)" title="John Tyndall (politician)">John Tyndall</a> and  a friend of Savitri Devi, who also attended the meeting. The National Socialist Movement wore quasi-SA uniforms,  was involved in streets conflicts with the Jewish <a href="/wiki/62_Group" title="62 Group">62 Group</a>. In the 1970s, Tyndall's earlier involvement with neo-Nazism would come back to haunt the <a href="/wiki/National_Front_(UK)" title="National Front (UK)">National Front</a>, which he led, as they attempted to ride a wave of anti-immigration populism and concerns over British national decline. Televised exposes on <i><a href="/wiki/This_Week_(Thames_Television_TV_series)" class="mw-redirect" title="This Week (Thames Television TV series)">This Week</a></i> in 1974 and <i><a href="/wiki/World_in_Action" title="World in Action">World in Action</a></i> in 1978, showed their neo-Nazi pedigree and damaged their electoral chances. In 1967, Rockwell was killed by a disgruntled former member. <a href="/wiki/Matthias_Koehl" title="Matthias Koehl">Matthias Koehl</a> took control of the ANP, and strongly influenced by Savitri Devi, gradually transformed it into an esoteric group known as the <a href="/wiki/New_Order_(Neo-Nazi_group)" class="mw-redirect" title="New Order (Neo-Nazi group)">New Order</a>.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">&#91;<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (December 2017)">citation needed</span></a></i>&#93;</sup>
</p>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Flag_of_Ordine_Nuovo.svg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/37/Flag_of_Ordine_Nuovo.svg/220px-Flag_of_Ordine_Nuovo.svg.png" width="220" height="147" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/37/Flag_of_Ordine_Nuovo.svg/330px-Flag_of_Ordine_Nuovo.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/37/Flag_of_Ordine_Nuovo.svg/440px-Flag_of_Ordine_Nuovo.svg.png 2x" data-file-width="600" data-file-height="400" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Flag_of_Ordine_Nuovo.svg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>The Italian group <a href="/wiki/Ordine_Nuovo" title="Ordine Nuovo">Ordine Nuovo</a>, banned in 1974, drew influence from the <a href="/wiki/Waffen-SS" title="Waffen-SS">Waffen-SS</a> and <a href="/wiki/Traditionalist_School" title="Traditionalist School">Guénonian Traditionalism</a> via <a href="/wiki/Julius_Evola" title="Julius Evola">Julius Evola</a>.</div></div></div>
<p>In <a href="/wiki/Franco%27s_Spain" class="mw-redirect" title="Franco&#39;s Spain">Franco's Spain</a>, certain SS refugees most notably <a href="/wiki/Otto_Skorzeny" title="Otto Skorzeny">Otto Skorzeny</a>, <a href="/wiki/L%C3%A9on_Degrelle" title="Léon Degrelle">Léon Degrelle</a> and the son of <a href="/wiki/Klaus_Barbie" title="Klaus Barbie">Klaus Barbie</a> became associated with <a href="/wiki/CEDADE" title="CEDADE">CEDADE</a> (<i>Círculo Español de Amigos de Europa</i>), an organisation which disseminated Third Reich apologetics out of <a href="/wiki/Barcelona" title="Barcelona">Barcelona</a>. They intersected with neo-Nazi advocates from <a href="/wiki/Mark_Fredriksen" title="Mark Fredriksen">Mark Fredriksen</a> in France to <a href="/wiki/Salvador_Borrego" title="Salvador Borrego">Salvador Borrego</a> in <a href="/wiki/Mexico" title="Mexico">Mexico</a>. 
In the post-fascist <a href="/wiki/Italian_Social_Movement" title="Italian Social Movement">Italian Social Movement</a> splinter groups such as <a href="/wiki/Ordine_Nuovo" title="Ordine Nuovo">Ordine Nuovo</a> and <a href="/wiki/Avanguardia_Nazionale" class="mw-redirect" title="Avanguardia Nazionale">Avanguardia Nazionale</a>, involved in the "<a href="/wiki/Years_of_Lead_(Italy)" title="Years of Lead (Italy)">Years of Lead</a>" considered National Socialism a reference. <a href="/wiki/Franco_Freda" title="Franco Freda">Franco Freda</a> created a "Nazi-<a href="/wiki/Maoist" class="mw-redirect" title="Maoist">Maoist</a>" synthesis. 
</p><p>In Germany itself, the various Third Reich nostalgic movements coalesced around the <a href="/wiki/National_Democratic_Party_of_Germany" title="National Democratic Party of Germany">National Democratic Party of Germany</a> in 1964 and in Austria the <a href="/wiki/National_Democratic_Party_(Austria,_1967%E2%80%9388)" title="National Democratic Party (Austria, 1967–88)">National Democratic Party</a> in 1967 as the primary sympathisers of the NSDAP past, although more publicly cautious than earlier groups.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">&#91;<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (December 2017)">citation needed</span></a></i>&#93;</sup>
</p>
<h3><span id="Holocaust_denial_and_subcultures.2C_1970s.E2.80.931990s"></span><span class="mw-headline" id="Holocaust_denial_and_subcultures,_1970s–1990s">Holocaust denial and subcultures, 1970s–1990s</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Neo-Nazism&amp;action=edit&amp;section=7" title="Edit section: Holocaust denial and subcultures, 1970s–1990s">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p><a href="/wiki/Holocaust_denial" title="Holocaust denial">Holocaust denial</a>, the claim that <a href="/wiki/The_Holocaust" title="The Holocaust">six million Jews</a> were not deliberately and systematically exterminated as an official policy of the Third Reich and Adolf Hitler, became a more prominent feature in the 1970s. Before this time, Holocaust denial had long existed as a sentiment among neo-Nazis, but had not yet been systematically articulated as a theory with a bibliographical canon. Few of the major theorists of Holocaust denial (who call themselves "<a href="/wiki/Historical_revisionism" title="Historical revisionism">revisionists</a>") can be uncontroversially classified as outright neo-Nazis (though some works such as that of <a href="/wiki/David_Irving" title="David Irving">David Irving</a> forward a clearly sympathetic view of Hitler and the publisher <a href="/wiki/Ernst_Z%C3%BCndel" title="Ernst Zündel">Ernst Zündel</a> was deeply tied to international neo-Nazism), however, the main interest of Holocaust denial to the neo-Nazi movement was their hope that it would help them to rehabilitate their political ideology in the eyes of the general public. <i><a href="/wiki/Did_Six_Million_Really_Die%3F" title="Did Six Million Really Die?">Did Six Million Really Die?</a></i> (1974) by <a href="/wiki/Richard_Verrall" title="Richard Verrall">Richard Verrall</a> and <i><a href="/wiki/The_Hoax_of_the_Twentieth_Century" title="The Hoax of the Twentieth Century">The Hoax of the Twentieth Century</a></i> (1976) by <a href="/wiki/Arthur_Butz" title="Arthur Butz">Arthur Butz</a> are popular examples of Holocaust denial material.
</p>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Flag_of_the_Order_of_Flemish_Militants.svg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/f4/Flag_of_the_Order_of_Flemish_Militants.svg/220px-Flag_of_the_Order_of_Flemish_Militants.svg.png" width="220" height="147" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/f4/Flag_of_the_Order_of_Flemish_Militants.svg/330px-Flag_of_the_Order_of_Flemish_Militants.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/f4/Flag_of_the_Order_of_Flemish_Militants.svg/440px-Flag_of_the_Order_of_Flemish_Militants.svg.png 2x" data-file-width="1200" data-file-height="800" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Flag_of_the_Order_of_Flemish_Militants.svg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>The radicalisation of Flemish activist group the <a href="/wiki/Vlaamse_Militanten_Orde" class="mw-redirect" title="Vlaamse Militanten Orde">Vlaamse Militanten Orde</a> in the 1970s, energised international neo-Nazism.</div></div></div>
<p>Key developments in international neo-Nazism during this time include the radicalisation of the <i><a href="/wiki/Vlaamse_Militanten_Orde" class="mw-redirect" title="Vlaamse Militanten Orde">Vlaamse Militanten Orde</a></i> under former Hitler Youth member <a href="/wiki/Bert_Eriksson" title="Bert Eriksson">Bert Eriksson</a>. They began hosting an annual conference; the "Iron Pilgrimage"; at <a href="/wiki/Diksmuide" title="Diksmuide">Diksmuide</a>, which drew kindred ideologues from across Europe and beyond. As well as this, the <a href="/wiki/NSDAP/AO_(1972)" title="NSDAP/AO (1972)">NSDAP/AO</a> under <a href="/wiki/Gary_Lauck" title="Gary Lauck">Gary Lauck</a> arose in the United States in 1972 and challenged the international influence of the Rockwellite WUNS. Lauck's organisation drew support from the <a href="/wiki/National_Socialist_Movement_of_Denmark" title="National Socialist Movement of Denmark">National Socialist Movement of Denmark</a> of <a href="/wiki/Povl_Riis-Knudsen" title="Povl Riis-Knudsen">Povl Riis-Knudsen</a> and various German and Austrian figures who felt that the "National Democratic" parties were too bourgeois and insufficiently National Socialist in orientation. This included <a href="/wiki/Michael_K%C3%BChnen" title="Michael Kühnen">Michael Kühnen</a>, <a href="/wiki/Christian_Worch" title="Christian Worch">Christian Worch</a>, <a href="/wiki/Bela_Ewald_Althans" title="Bela Ewald Althans">Bela Ewald Althans</a> and <a href="/wiki/Gottfried_K%C3%BCssel" title="Gottfried Küssel">Gottfried Küssel</a> of the 1977-founded  <a href="/wiki/Action_Front_of_National_Socialists/National_Activists" title="Action Front of National Socialists/National Activists">ANS/NS</a> which called for the establishment of a Germanic <a href="/wiki/Fourth_Reich" title="Fourth Reich">Fourth Reich</a>. Some ANS/NS members were imprisoned for planning paramilitary attacks on <a href="/wiki/NATO" title="NATO">NATO</a> bases in Germany and planning to liberate <a href="/wiki/Rudolf_Hess" title="Rudolf Hess">Rudolf Hess</a> from <a href="/wiki/Spandau_Prison" title="Spandau Prison">Spandau Prison</a>. The organisation was officially banned in 1983 by the Minister of the Interior.
</p><p>During the late 1970s, a British subculture came to be associated with neo-Nazism; the <a href="/wiki/Skinheads" class="mw-redirect" title="Skinheads">skinheads</a>. Portraying an ultra-masculine, crude and aggressive image, with working-class references, some of the skinheads joined the <a href="/wiki/British_Movement" title="British Movement">British Movement</a> under <a href="/wiki/Michael_McLaughlin" title="Michael McLaughlin">Michael McLaughlin</a> (successor of Colin Jordan), while others became associated with the National Front's <a href="/wiki/Rock_Against_Communism" title="Rock Against Communism">Rock Against Communism</a> project which was meant to counter the <a href="/wiki/Socialist_Workers_Party_(UK)" title="Socialist Workers Party (UK)">SWP</a>'s <a href="/wiki/Rock_Against_Racism" title="Rock Against Racism">Rock Against Racism</a>. The most significant music group involved was <a href="/wiki/Skrewdriver" title="Skrewdriver">Skrewdriver</a>, led by <a href="/wiki/Ian_Stuart_Donaldson" title="Ian Stuart Donaldson">Ian Stuart Donaldson</a>. Together with ex-BM member <a href="/wiki/Nicky_Crane" title="Nicky Crane">Nicky Crane</a>, Donaldson founded the international <a href="/wiki/Blood_%26_Honour" title="Blood &amp; Honour">Blood &amp; Honour</a> network in 1987. By 1992 this network, with input from <a href="/wiki/Harold_Covington" title="Harold Covington">Harold Covington</a>, had developed a paramilitary wing; <a href="/wiki/Combat_18" title="Combat 18">Combat 18</a>, which intersected with football hooligan firms such as the <a href="/wiki/Chelsea_Headhunters" title="Chelsea Headhunters">Chelsea Headhunters</a>. The neo-Nazi skinhead movement spread to the United States, with groups such as the <a href="/wiki/Hammerskins" title="Hammerskins">Hammerskins</a>. It was popularised from 1986 onwards by <a href="/wiki/Tom_Metzger" title="Tom Metzger">Tom Metzger</a> of the <a href="/wiki/White_Aryan_Resistance" title="White Aryan Resistance">White Aryan Resistance</a>. Since then it has spread across the world. Films such as <i><a href="/wiki/Romper_Stomper" title="Romper Stomper">Romper Stomper</a></i> (1992) and <i><a href="/wiki/American_History_X" title="American History X">American History X</a></i> (1998) would fix a public perception of <a href="/wiki/White_power_skinheads" class="mw-redirect" title="White power skinheads">neo-Nazism and skinheads</a> being synonymous.
</p>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:202px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Black_Sun_2.svg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c5/Black_Sun_2.svg/200px-Black_Sun_2.svg.png" width="200" height="200" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c5/Black_Sun_2.svg/300px-Black_Sun_2.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c5/Black_Sun_2.svg/400px-Black_Sun_2.svg.png 2x" data-file-width="512" data-file-height="512" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Black_Sun_2.svg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Serrano identified Aryan-Hyperborean blood as the "light of the <a href="/wiki/Black_Sun_(occult_symbol)" class="mw-redirect" title="Black Sun (occult symbol)">Black Sun</a>", a symbol found at SS-cult site <a href="/wiki/Wewelsburg" title="Wewelsburg">Wewelsburg Castle</a>.</div></div></div>
<p>New developments also emerged on the esoteric level, as former Chilean diplomat <a href="/wiki/Miguel_Serrano" title="Miguel Serrano">Miguel Serrano</a> built on the works of <a href="/wiki/Carl_Jung" title="Carl Jung">Carl Jung</a>, <a href="/wiki/Otto_Rahn" title="Otto Rahn">Otto Rahn</a>, Wilhelm Landig, Julius Evola and Savitri Devi to bind together and develop already existing theories. Serrano had been a member of the <a href="/wiki/National_Socialist_Movement_of_Chile" title="National Socialist Movement of Chile">National Socialist Movement of Chile</a> in the 1930s and from the early days of neo-Nazism had been in contact with key figures across Europe and beyond. Despite this, he was able to work as an ambassador to numerous countries until the rise of <a href="/wiki/Salvador_Allende" title="Salvador Allende">Salvador Allende</a>. In 1984 he published his book <i>Adolf Hitler: The Ultimate Avatar</i>. Serrano claimed that the <a href="/wiki/Aryans" class="mw-redirect" title="Aryans">Aryans</a> were extragalactic beings who founded <a href="/wiki/Hyperborea" title="Hyperborea">Hyperborea</a> and lived the heroic life of <a href="/wiki/Bodhisattvas" class="mw-redirect" title="Bodhisattvas">Bodhisattvas</a>, while the <a href="/wiki/Jews" title="Jews">Jews</a> were created by the <a href="/wiki/Demiurge" title="Demiurge">Demiurge</a> and were concerned only with coarse <a href="/wiki/Materialism" title="Materialism">materialism</a>. Serrano claimed that a new <a href="/wiki/Golden_Age" title="Golden Age">Golden Age</a> can be attained if the Hyperboreans repurify their blood (supposedly the light of the Black Sun) and restore their "<a href="/wiki/Genetic_memory_(psychology)" title="Genetic memory (psychology)">blood-memory</a>." As with Savitri Devi before him, Serrano's works became a key point of reference in neo-Nazism.
</p>
<h3><span id="Lifting_of_the_Iron_Curtain.2C_1990s.E2.80.93present"></span><span class="mw-headline" id="Lifting_of_the_Iron_Curtain,_1990s–present">Lifting of the Iron Curtain, 1990s–present</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Neo-Nazism&amp;action=edit&amp;section=8" title="Edit section: Lifting of the Iron Curtain, 1990s–present">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>With the fall of the <a href="/wiki/Berlin_Wall" title="Berlin Wall">Berlin Wall</a> and the <a href="/wiki/Collapse_of_the_Soviet_Union" class="mw-redirect" title="Collapse of the Soviet Union">collapse of the Soviet Union</a> during the early 1990s, neo-Nazism began to spread its ideas in the East, as hostility to the triumphant liberal order was high and <a href="/wiki/Revanchism" title="Revanchism">revanchism</a> a widespread feeling. In Russia, during the chaos of the early 1990s, an amorphous mixture of <a href="/wiki/KGB" title="KGB">KGB</a> hardliners, Orthodox neo-Tsarist nostalgics (i.e. - <a href="/wiki/Pamyat" title="Pamyat">Pamyat</a>) and explicit National Socialists found themselves strewn together in the same camp. They were united by opposition to the influence of the United States, against the liberalising legacy of <a href="/wiki/Mikhail_Gorbachev" title="Mikhail Gorbachev">Mikhail Gorbachev</a>'s <i><a href="/wiki/Perestroika" title="Perestroika">perestroika</a></i> and on the <a href="/wiki/Jewish_question" title="Jewish question">Jewish question</a>, <a href="/wiki/Soviet_Anti-Zionism" class="mw-redirect" title="Soviet Anti-Zionism">Soviet Zionology</a> merged with a more explicit anti-Jewish sentiment. The most significant organisation representing this was <a href="/wiki/Russian_National_Unity" title="Russian National Unity">Russian National Unity</a> under the leadership of <a href="/wiki/Alexander_Barkashov" title="Alexander Barkashov">Alexander Barkashov</a>, where black-uniform clad Russians marched with a red flag incorporating the <a href="/wiki/Swastika" title="Swastika">Swastika</a> under the banner of <i><a href="/wiki/Russia_for_Russians" title="Russia for Russians">Russia for Russians</a>.</i> These forces came together in a last gasp effort to save the <a href="/wiki/Supreme_Soviet_of_Russia" title="Supreme Soviet of Russia">Supreme Soviet of Russia</a> against <a href="/wiki/Boris_Yeltsin" title="Boris Yeltsin">Boris Yeltsin</a> during the <a href="/wiki/1993_Russian_constitutional_crisis" title="1993 Russian constitutional crisis">1993 Russian constitutional crisis</a>. As well as events in Russia, in newly independent ex-Soviet states, annual commemorations for SS volunteers now took place; particularly in <a href="/wiki/Remembrance_day_of_the_Latvian_legionnaires" title="Remembrance day of the Latvian legionnaires">Latvia</a>, <a href="/wiki/20th_Waffen_Grenadier_Division_of_the_SS_(1st_Estonian)" title="20th Waffen Grenadier Division of the SS (1st Estonian)">Estonia</a> and the <a href="/wiki/14th_Waffen_Grenadier_Division_of_the_SS_(1st_Galician)" title="14th Waffen Grenadier Division of the SS (1st Galician)">Ukraine</a>.
</p>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Evstafiev-neo-bolsheviks.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/91/Evstafiev-neo-bolsheviks.jpg/220px-Evstafiev-neo-bolsheviks.jpg" width="220" height="146" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/91/Evstafiev-neo-bolsheviks.jpg/330px-Evstafiev-neo-bolsheviks.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/91/Evstafiev-neo-bolsheviks.jpg/440px-Evstafiev-neo-bolsheviks.jpg 2x" data-file-width="500" data-file-height="331" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Evstafiev-neo-bolsheviks.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Members of the <a href="/wiki/National_Bolshevik_Party" title="National Bolshevik Party">National Bolshevik Party</a>. The Nazbols tailored ultra-nationalist themes to a native Russian environment, while still flirting with National Socialist aesthetics.</div></div></div>
<p>The Russian developments excited German neo-Nazism who dreamed of a <a href="/wiki/Berlin" title="Berlin">Berlin</a>—<a href="/wiki/Moscow" title="Moscow">Moscow</a> alliance against the supposedly "decadent" <a href="/wiki/Atlanticist" class="mw-redirect" title="Atlanticist">Atlanticist</a> forces; a dream which had been thematic since the days of Remer.<sup id="cite_ref-remer_11-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-remer-11">&#91;11&#93;</a></sup> Zündel visited Russia and met with Russian National Unity luminaries such as ex-KGB general Aleksandr Stergilov. Despite these initial aspirations, international neo-Nazism and its close affiliates in ultra-nationalism would be split over the <a href="/wiki/Bosnian_War" title="Bosnian War">Bosnian War</a> between 1992 and 1995, as part of the <a href="/wiki/Breakup_of_Yugoslavia" title="Breakup of Yugoslavia">breakup of Yugoslavia</a>. The split would largely be along ethnic and sectarian lines. The Germans and the French would largely back the Western Catholic <a href="/wiki/Croats" title="Croats">Croats</a> (Lauck's NSDAP/AO explicitly <a href="/wiki/Foreign_fighters_in_the_Bosnian_War" title="Foreign fighters in the Bosnian War">called for volunteers</a>, which Kühnen's <a href="/wiki/Free_German_Workers%27_Party" title="Free German Workers&#39; Party">Free German Workers' Party</a> answered and the French formed the "Groupe <a href="/wiki/Jacques_Doriot" title="Jacques Doriot">Jacques Doriot</a>"), while the Russians and the Greeks would back the Orthodox <a href="/wiki/Serbs" title="Serbs">Serbs</a> (including Russians from Barkashov's Russian National Unity, <a href="/wiki/Eduard_Limonov" title="Eduard Limonov">Eduard Limonov</a>'s <a href="/wiki/National_Bolshevik_Party" title="National Bolshevik Party">National Bolshevik Front</a> and <a href="/wiki/Golden_Dawn_(political_party)" title="Golden Dawn (political party)">Golden Dawn</a> members joined the <a href="/wiki/Greek_Volunteer_Guard" title="Greek Volunteer Guard">Greek Volunteer Guard</a>). Indeed, the revival of <a href="/wiki/National_Bolshevism" title="National Bolshevism">National Bolshevism</a> was able to steal some of the thunder from overt Russian neo-Nazism, as ultra-nationalism was wedded with veneration of <a href="/wiki/Joseph_Stalin" title="Joseph Stalin">Joseph Stalin</a> in place of Adolf Hitler, while still also flirting with National Socialist aesthetics.
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Analogous_European_movements">Analogous European movements</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Neo-Nazism&amp;action=edit&amp;section=9" title="Edit section: Analogous European movements">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<p>Outside Germany, in other countries which were involved with the <a href="/wiki/Axis_powers" title="Axis powers">Axis powers</a> and had their own native ultra-nationalist movements, which sometimes collaborated with the Third Reich but were not technically German-style National Socialists, revivalist and nostalgic movements have emerged in the post-war period which, as neo-Nazism has done in Germany, seek to rehabilitate their various loosely associated ideologies. These movements include <a href="/wiki/Neo-fascists" class="mw-redirect" title="Neo-fascists">neo-fascists</a> and <a href="/wiki/Post-fascists" class="mw-redirect" title="Post-fascists">post-fascists</a> in Italy; Vichyites, Pétainists and "national Europeans" in France; <a href="/wiki/Usta%C5%A1e" class="mw-redirect" title="Ustaše">Ustaše</a> sympathisers in <a href="/wiki/Croatia" title="Croatia">Croatia</a>; neo-<a href="/wiki/Chetniks" title="Chetniks">Chetniks</a> in <a href="/wiki/Serbia" title="Serbia">Serbia</a>; <a href="/wiki/Iron_Guard" title="Iron Guard">Iron Guard</a> revivalists in <a href="/wiki/Romania" title="Romania">Romania</a>; <a href="/wiki/Hungarists" class="mw-redirect" title="Hungarists">Hungarists</a> and <a href="/wiki/Mikl%C3%B3s_Horthy" title="Miklós Horthy">Horthyists</a> in <a href="/wiki/Hungary" title="Hungary">Hungary</a>; <a href="/wiki/Stepan_Bandera" title="Stepan Bandera">Banderaists</a> in the <a href="/wiki/Ukraine" title="Ukraine">Ukraine</a> (which had a complicated relationship with the Axis powers) and others.
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Italy">Italy</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Neo-Nazism&amp;action=edit&amp;section=10" title="Edit section: Italy">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Junio_Valerio_Borghese.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/80/Junio_Valerio_Borghese.jpg/220px-Junio_Valerio_Borghese.jpg" width="220" height="325" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/8/80/Junio_Valerio_Borghese.jpg 1.5x" data-file-width="224" data-file-height="331" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Junio_Valerio_Borghese.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>The nearest Italy came to returning to <a href="/wiki/Fascism" title="Fascism">fascism</a> was the 1970 <a href="/wiki/Golpe_Borghese" title="Golpe Borghese">Golpe Borghese</a> of commando veteran <a href="/wiki/Junio_Valerio_Borghese" title="Junio Valerio Borghese">Junio Valerio Borghese</a>.</div></div></div>
<p>Following the last stand of <a href="/wiki/Italian_fascism" class="mw-redirect" title="Italian fascism">Italian fascism</a> with the German-supported <a href="/wiki/Italian_Social_Republic" title="Italian Social Republic">Italian Social Republic</a> towards the end of the Second World War, those elements within Italian society which remained loyal to the legacy of <a href="/wiki/Benito_Mussolini" title="Benito Mussolini">Benito Mussolini</a> and fascism (especially veterans of the <a href="/wiki/National_Republican_Army" title="National Republican Army">National Republican Army</a>), rejecting both the <a href="/wiki/Christian_Democracy_(Italy)" title="Christian Democracy (Italy)">Catholic</a> and <a href="/wiki/Italian_Communist_Party" title="Italian Communist Party">Communist</a> alternatives prominent in mainstream Italian politics, founded the <a href="/wiki/Italian_Social_Movement" title="Italian Social Movement">Italian Social Movement</a> in 1946 under <a href="/wiki/Giorgio_Almirante" title="Giorgio Almirante">Giorgio Almirante</a>. The MSI was regarded as the successor of the <a href="/wiki/National_Fascist_Party" title="National Fascist Party">National Fascist Party</a> and the <a href="/wiki/Republican_Fascist_Party" title="Republican Fascist Party">Republican Fascist Party</a>. The motto of the party was "not repudiate, not restore", indicating a more moderate parliamentary democratic <a href="/wiki/Neo-fascism" title="Neo-fascism">neo-fascism</a>, which did not heap scorn on the recent past. Italian society did not undergo a process as extensive as the post-war Denazification campaign in occupied Germany, partly due to the <a href="/wiki/Cold_War" title="Cold War">Cold War</a> and the Western Allies not wanting Italy to move towards the <a href="/wiki/Warsaw_Pact" title="Warsaw Pact">Warsaw Pact</a> (which was not an <a href="/wiki/Popular_Democratic_Front_(Italy)" title="Popular Democratic Front (Italy)">impossibility at the time</a>).<sup id="cite_ref-18" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-18">&#91;16&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-19" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-19">&#91;17&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>The Italian Social Movement held a similar position in Italian politics that the <a href="/wiki/National_Democratic_Party_of_Germany" title="National Democratic Party of Germany">National Democratic Party of Germany</a> did in Germany; careful enough to stay within the laws of the new democratic state, but still clearly identified with the Axis legacy. During the 1950s, the MSI moved closer to bourgeois conservative politics on the domestic front, which led to radical youths founding hardline splinter groups, such as <a href="/wiki/Pino_Rauti" title="Pino Rauti">Pino Rauti</a>'s <a href="/wiki/Ordine_Nuovo" title="Ordine Nuovo">Ordine Nuovo</a> (later succeeded by <a href="/wiki/Ordine_Nero" title="Ordine Nero">Ordine Nero</a>) and  <a href="/wiki/Stefano_Delle_Chiaie" title="Stefano Delle Chiaie">Stefano Delle Chiaie</a>'s <a href="/wiki/National_Vanguard_(Italy)" title="National Vanguard (Italy)">Avanguardia Nazionale</a>. These organisations were influenced by the esotericism of <a href="/wiki/Julius_Evola" title="Julius Evola">Julius Evola</a> and considered the Waffen-SS and Romanian leader <a href="/wiki/Corneliu_Zelea_Codreanu" title="Corneliu Zelea Codreanu">Corneliu Zelea Codreanu</a> a reference, moving beyond Italian fascism. They were implicated in <a href="/wiki/Years_of_Lead_(Italy)" title="Years of Lead (Italy)">paramiliary attacks</a> during the late 1960s to the early 1980s, such as the <a href="/wiki/Piazza_Fontana_bombing" title="Piazza Fontana bombing">Piazza Fontana bombing</a>. Delle Chiaie had even assisted <a href="/wiki/Junio_Valerio_Borghese" title="Junio Valerio Borghese">Junio Valerio Borghese</a> in a failed 1970 coup attempt known as the <a href="/wiki/Golpe_Borghese" title="Golpe Borghese">Golpe Borghese</a>, which attempted to reinstate a fascist state in Italy.
</p><p>During the late 1980s and early 1990s, the Italian Social Movement under the leadership of <a href="/wiki/Gianfranco_Fini" title="Gianfranco Fini">Gianfranco Fini</a> moved closer to conservative politics, adopting a "post-fascist" position. This was opposed by the fascist element under Rauti who created <a href="/wiki/Fiamma_Tricolore" class="mw-redirect" title="Fiamma Tricolore">Fiamma Tricolore</a> in 1995. The party was dissolved under Fini in 1995, who replaced it with the <a href="/wiki/National_Alliance_(Italy)" title="National Alliance (Italy)">National Alliance</a>. This party rapidly moved away from any connection to the fascist past, towards the center-right in coalition with <a href="/wiki/Silvio_Berlusconi" title="Silvio Berlusconi">Silvio Berlusconi</a>'s <a href="/wiki/Forza_Italia" title="Forza Italia">Forza Italia</a>. The two parties merged in 2009 to become <a href="/wiki/The_People_of_Freedom" title="The People of Freedom">The People of Freedom</a>. <a href="/wiki/Alessandra_Mussolini" title="Alessandra Mussolini">Alessandra Mussolini</a>, troubled by Fini's explicit condemnation of her grandfather broke with the AN to found <a href="/wiki/Social_Action" title="Social Action">Social Action</a>. Aside from Fiamma Tricolore, the other extant neo-fascist groups in Italy are <a href="/wiki/New_Force_(Italy)" title="New Force (Italy)">Forza Nuova</a>, the <a href="/wiki/National_Social_Front" class="mw-redirect" title="National Social Front">Fronte Nazionale</a>, <a href="/wiki/Social_Idea_Movement" title="Social Idea Movement">Movimento Idea Sociale</a> (another Rauti creation) and the cultural <i><a href="/wiki/CasaPound" title="CasaPound">CasaPound</a></i> project. In terms of current size, they are mostly negligible.
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="France">France</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Neo-Nazism&amp;action=edit&amp;section=11" title="Edit section: France">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Main article: <a href="/wiki/History_of_far-right_movements_in_France" title="History of far-right movements in France">History of far-right movements in France</a></div>
<div class="thumb tleft"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:202px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Celtic-style_crossed_circle.svg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d2/Celtic-style_crossed_circle.svg/200px-Celtic-style_crossed_circle.svg.png" width="200" height="200" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d2/Celtic-style_crossed_circle.svg/300px-Celtic-style_crossed_circle.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d2/Celtic-style_crossed_circle.svg/400px-Celtic-style_crossed_circle.svg.png 2x" data-file-width="62" data-file-height="62" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Celtic-style_crossed_circle.svg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>French neo-fascist groups adopted the <a href="/wiki/Celtic_cross" title="Celtic cross">Celtic cross</a> as an ambiguous "Christian and pagan" symbol since the 1940s.</div></div></div>
<p>In France, the most enthusiastic collaborationists during the <a href="/wiki/German_military_administration_in_occupied_France_during_World_War_II" title="German military administration in occupied France during World War II">German occupation of France</a> had been the <a href="/wiki/National_Popular_Rally" title="National Popular Rally">National Popular Rally</a> of <a href="/wiki/Marcel_D%C3%A9at" title="Marcel Déat">Marcel Déat</a> (former <a href="/wiki/French_Section_of_the_Workers%27_International" title="French Section of the Workers&#39; International">SFIO</a> members) and the <a href="/wiki/French_Popular_Party" title="French Popular Party">French Popular Party</a> of <a href="/wiki/Jacques_Doriot" title="Jacques Doriot">Jacques Doriot</a> (former <a href="/wiki/French_Communist_Party" title="French Communist Party">French Communist Party</a> members). These two groups, like the Germans, saw themselves as combining ultra-nationalism and <a href="/wiki/Socialism" title="Socialism">socialism</a>. In the south there existed the vassal state of <a href="/wiki/Vichy_France" title="Vichy France">Vichy France</a> under the military "Hero of the Verdun", Marshal <a href="/wiki/Philippe_P%C3%A9tain" title="Philippe Pétain">Philippe Pétain</a> whose <i><a href="/wiki/R%C3%A9volution_nationale" title="Révolution nationale">Révolution nationale</a></i> emphasised an authoritarian Catholic conservative politics. Following the <a href="/wiki/Liberation_of_France" class="mw-redirect" title="Liberation of France">liberation of France</a> and the creation of the <a href="/wiki/Fourth_French_Republic" class="mw-redirect" title="Fourth French Republic">Fourth French Republic</a>, collaborationists were prosecuted during the <i><a href="/wiki/%C3%89puration_l%C3%A9gale" title="Épuration légale">épuration légale</a></i> and nearly 800 put to death for treason under <a href="/wiki/Charles_de_Gaulle" title="Charles de Gaulle">Charles de Gaulle</a>.
</p><p>In the aftermath of the Second World War, the main concern of the French radical right was the collapse of the <a href="/wiki/French_colonial_empire" title="French colonial empire">French Empire</a>, in particular the <a href="/wiki/Algerian_War" title="Algerian War">Algerian War</a>, which led to the creation of the <a href="/wiki/Organisation_arm%C3%A9e_secr%C3%A8te" title="Organisation armée secrète">OAS</a>. Outside of this, individual fascistic activists such as <a href="/wiki/Maurice_Bard%C3%A8che" title="Maurice Bardèche">Maurice Bardèche</a> (brother-in-law of <a href="/wiki/Robert_Brasillach" title="Robert Brasillach">Robert Brasillach</a>), as well as SS-veterans <a href="/wiki/Marc_Augier" title="Marc Augier">Saint-Loup</a> and <a href="/wiki/Ren%C3%A9_Binet_(neo-Fascist)" title="René Binet (neo-Fascist)">René Binet</a>,  were active in France and involved in the <a href="/wiki/European_Social_Movement" title="European Social Movement">European Social Movement</a> and later the <a href="/wiki/New_European_Order" title="New European Order">New European Order</a>, alongside similar groups from across Europe. Early neo-fascist groups included <a href="/wiki/Jeune_Nation" title="Jeune Nation">Jeune Nation</a>, which introduced the <a href="/wiki/Celtic_cross" title="Celtic cross">Celtic cross</a> into use by radical right groups (an association which would spread internationally). A "neither East, nor West" pan-Europeanism was most popular among French fascistic activists until the late 1960s, partly motivated by feelings of national vulnerability following the collapse of their empire; thus the Belgian SS-veteran <a href="/wiki/Jean-Fran%C3%A7ois_Thiriart" title="Jean-François Thiriart">Jean-François Thiriart</a>'s group <a href="/wiki/Jeune_Europe" title="Jeune Europe">Jeune Europe</a> also had a considerable French contingent.
</p><p>It was the 1960s, during the <a href="/wiki/Fifth_French_Republic" class="mw-redirect" title="Fifth French Republic">Fifth French Republic</a>, that a considerable upturn in French neo-fascism occurred; some of it in response to the <a href="/wiki/Protests_of_1968" title="Protests of 1968">Protests of 1968</a>. The most explicitly pro-Nazi of these was the <a href="/wiki/F%C3%A9d%C3%A9ration_d%27action_nationale_et_europ%C3%A9enne" title="Fédération d&#39;action nationale et européenne">FANE</a> of <a href="/wiki/Mark_Fredriksen" title="Mark Fredriksen">Mark Fredriksen</a>. Neo-fascist groups included <a href="/wiki/Pierre_Sidos" title="Pierre Sidos">Pierre Sidos</a>' <a href="/wiki/Occident_(movement)" title="Occident (movement)">Occident</a>, the <a href="/wiki/Ordre_Nouveau_(1960s)" title="Ordre Nouveau (1960s)">Ordre Nouveau</a> (which was banned after violent clashes with the Trotskyist <a href="/wiki/Revolutionary_Communist_League_(France)" title="Revolutionary Communist League (France)">LCR</a>) and the student-based <a href="/wiki/Groupe_Union_D%C3%A9fense" title="Groupe Union Défense">Groupe Union Défense</a>. A number of these activists such as <a href="/wiki/Fran%C3%A7ois_Duprat" title="François Duprat">François Duprat</a> were instrumental in founding the <a href="/wiki/National_Front_(France)" class="mw-redirect" title="National Front (France)">Front National</a> under <a href="/wiki/Jean-Marie_Le_Pen" title="Jean-Marie Le Pen">Jean-Marie Le Pen</a>; but the FN also included a broader selection from the French hard-right, including not only these neo-fascist elements, but also <a href="/wiki/Integrism" title="Integrism">Catholic integrists</a>, monarchists, Algerian War veterans, <a href="/wiki/Poujadists" class="mw-redirect" title="Poujadists">Poujadists</a> and national-conservatives. Others from these neo-fascist micro-groups formed the <a href="/wiki/Parti_des_forces_nouvelles" class="mw-redirect" title="Parti des forces nouvelles">Parti des forces nouvelles</a> working against Le Pen.
</p><p>Within the FN itself, Duprat founded the FANE-backed <a href="/wiki/Revolutionary_Nationalist_Groups" title="Revolutionary Nationalist Groups">Groupes nationalistes révolutionnaires</a> faction, until his 1978 assassination. The subsequent history of the French hard right has been the conflict between the national-conservative controlled FN and "national revolutionary" (fascistic and National Bolshevik) splinter or opposition groups. The latter include groups in the tradition of Thiriart and Duprat, such as the <a href="/wiki/Parti_communautaire_national-europ%C3%A9en" class="mw-redirect" title="Parti communautaire national-européen">Parti communautaire national-européen</a>, <a href="/wiki/Troisi%C3%A8me_voie" class="mw-redirect" title="Troisième voie">Troisième voie</a>, the <a href="/wiki/Nouvelle_R%C3%A9sistance" title="Nouvelle Résistance">Nouvelle Résistance</a> of <a href="/wiki/Christian_Bouchet" title="Christian Bouchet">Christian Bouchet</a>,<sup id="cite_ref-Banquet_20-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Banquet-20">&#91;18&#93;</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Unit%C3%A9_Radicale" title="Unité Radicale">Unité Radicale</a> and most recently <a href="/wiki/Bloc_identitaire" class="mw-redirect" title="Bloc identitaire">Bloc identitaire</a>. Direct splits from the FN include the 1987 founded FANE-revival <a href="/wiki/Parti_nationaliste_fran%C3%A7ais_et_europ%C3%A9en" class="mw-redirect" title="Parti nationaliste français et européen">Parti nationaliste français et européen</a>, which was disbanded in 2000. Neo-Nazi organizations are outlawed in the Fifth French Republic, yet a significant number of them still exist.<sup id="cite_ref-21" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-21">&#91;19&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Croatia">Croatia</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Neo-Nazism&amp;action=edit&amp;section=12" title="Edit section: Croatia">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">See also: <a href="/wiki/Far-right_politics_in_Croatia" title="Far-right politics in Croatia">Far-right politics in Croatia</a></div>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:252px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Thompson_Maksimir_17.6.2007_2.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/1c/Thompson_Maksimir_17.6.2007_2.jpg/250px-Thompson_Maksimir_17.6.2007_2.jpg" width="250" height="167" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/1c/Thompson_Maksimir_17.6.2007_2.jpg/375px-Thompson_Maksimir_17.6.2007_2.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/1c/Thompson_Maksimir_17.6.2007_2.jpg/500px-Thompson_Maksimir_17.6.2007_2.jpg 2x" data-file-width="720" data-file-height="480" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Thompson_Maksimir_17.6.2007_2.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Young boy wearing a shirt with a <a href="/wiki/Black_Legion_(Usta%C5%A1e_militia)" title="Black Legion (Ustaše militia)">Black Legion</a> sign at a <a href="/wiki/Thompson_(band)" title="Thompson (band)">Thompson</a> concert</div></div></div>
<p>Neo-Nazis in <a href="/wiki/Croatia" title="Croatia">Croatia</a> base their ideology on the writings of  <a href="/wiki/Ante_Paveli%C4%87" title="Ante Pavelić">Ante Pavelić</a> and the <a href="/wiki/Usta%C5%A1e" class="mw-redirect" title="Ustaše">Ustaše</a>, a <a href="/wiki/Fascism" title="Fascism">fascist</a> anti-Yugoslav separatist movement.<sup id="cite_ref-22" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-22">&#91;20&#93;</a></sup> The Ustaše regime committed a <a href="/wiki/Genocides_in_history#Croatia" title="Genocides in history">genocide</a> against <a href="/wiki/World_War_II_persecution_of_Serbs" class="mw-redirect" title="World War II persecution of Serbs">Serbs</a>, <a href="/wiki/The_Holocaust_in_the_Independent_State_of_Croatia" title="The Holocaust in the Independent State of Croatia">Jews and Roma</a>. At the end of <a href="/wiki/World_War_II" title="World War II">World War II</a>, many Ustaše members fled to <a href="/wiki/Western_World" class="mw-redirect" title="Western World">the West</a>, where they found sanctuary and continued their <a href="/wiki/Politics" title="Politics">political</a> and <a href="/wiki/Terrorism" title="Terrorism">terrorist</a> activities (which were tolerated due to <a href="/wiki/Cold_War" title="Cold War">Cold War</a> hostilities).<sup id="cite_ref-23" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-23">&#91;21&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-24" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-24">&#91;22&#93;</a></sup> 
</p><p>In 1999, Zagreb's <a href="/wiki/Square_of_the_Victims_of_Fascism" title="Square of the Victims of Fascism">Square of the Victims of Fascism</a> was renamed <i>Croatian Nobles Square</i>, provoking widespread criticism of Croatia's attitude towards the <a href="/wiki/Holocaust" class="mw-redirect" title="Holocaust">Holocaust</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-25" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-25">&#91;23&#93;</a></sup><sup class="noprint Inline-Template"><span style="white-space: nowrap;">&#91;<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Link_rot" title="Wikipedia:Link rot"><span title="&#160;Dead link since January 2018">dead link</span></a></i>&#93;</span></sup> In 2000, the <a href="/wiki/Zagreb_City_Council" class="mw-redirect" title="Zagreb City Council">Zagreb City Council</a> again renamed the sqare into <i>Square of the Victims of Fascism</i>.<sup id="cite_ref-26" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-26">&#91;24&#93;</a></sup> Many streets in Croatia were renamed after the prominent Ustaše figure <a href="/wiki/Mile_Budak" title="Mile Budak">Mile Budak</a>, which provoked outrage amongst the Serbian minority. Since 2002, there has been a reversal of this development, and streets with the name of Mile Budak or other persons connected with the Ustaše movement are few or non-existent.<sup id="cite_ref-news.bbc.co.uk_27-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-news.bbc.co.uk-27">&#91;25&#93;</a></sup> A plaque in <a href="/wiki/Slunj" title="Slunj">Slunj</a> with the inscription "Croatian Knight <a href="/wiki/Jure_Franceti%C4%87" title="Jure Francetić">Jure Francetić</a>" was erected to commemorate Francetić, the notorious Ustaše leader of the Black Legion. The plaque remained there for four years, until it was removed by the authorities.<sup id="cite_ref-news.bbc.co.uk_27-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-news.bbc.co.uk-27">&#91;25&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-28" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-28">&#91;26&#93;</a></sup> 
</p><p>In 2003, Croatian <a href="/wiki/Penal_code" class="mw-redirect" title="Penal code">penal code</a> was amended with provisions prohibiting the public display of Nazi symbols, the propagation of Nazi ideology, <a href="/wiki/Historical_revisionism" title="Historical revisionism">historical revisionism</a> and <a href="/wiki/Holocaust_denial" title="Holocaust denial">holocaust denial</a> but the amendments were annulled in 2004 since they weren't enacted in accordance with a constitutionally prescribed procedure.<sup id="cite_ref-29" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-29">&#91;27&#93;</a></sup> Nevertheless, since 2006 Croatian penal code explicitly prohibits any type of <a href="/wiki/Hate_crime" title="Hate crime">hate crime</a> based on  <a href="/wiki/Race_(classification_of_human_beings)" class="mw-redirect" title="Race (classification of human beings)">race</a>, <a href="/wiki/Human_skin_color" title="Human skin color">color</a>, <a href="/wiki/Gender" title="Gender">gender</a>, <a href="/wiki/Sexual_orientation" title="Sexual orientation">sexual orientation</a>, <a href="/wiki/Religion" title="Religion">religion</a> or national origin.<sup id="cite_ref-30" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-30">&#91;28&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>There have been instances of <a href="/wiki/Hate_speech" title="Hate speech">hate speech</a> in Croatia, such as the use of the phrase <i><a href="/wiki/Srbe_na_vrbe!" class="mw-redirect" title="Srbe na vrbe!">Srbe na vrbe!</a></i> ("(Hang) Serbs on the <a href="/wiki/Willow" title="Willow">willow</a> trees!").<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">&#91;<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (January 2018)">citation needed</span></a></i>&#93;</sup> In 2004, an <a href="/wiki/Eastern_Orthodoxy_in_Croatia" title="Eastern Orthodoxy in Croatia">Orthodox</a> church was <a href="/wiki/Spray_painting" title="Spray painting">spray-painted</a> with pro-Ustaše graffiti.<sup id="cite_ref-31" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-31">&#91;29&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-32" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-32">&#91;30&#93;</a></sup> During some protests in Croatia, supporters of <a href="/wiki/Ante_Gotovina" title="Ante Gotovina">Ante Gotovina</a> and other at the time suspected <a href="/wiki/War_crime" title="War crime">war criminals</a> (all acquitted in 2012) have carried <a href="/wiki/Nationalism" title="Nationalism">nationalist</a> symbols and pictures of Pavelić.<sup id="cite_ref-33" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-33">&#91;31&#93;</a></sup> On 17 May 2007, a concert in Zagreb by <a href="/wiki/Thompson_(band)" title="Thompson (band)">Thompson</a>, a popular Croatian singer, was attended by 60,000 people, some of them wearing Ustaše uniforms. Some gave Ustaše salutes and shouted the Ustaše slogan "<a href="/wiki/Za_dom_spremni" title="Za dom spremni">Za dom spremni</a>" (For the homeland – ready!).
